# Chapter 1

## The Importance of Focused Conversation for Schools

*Thinking, questioning and imagining are the tools of human consciousness, the things that make us different from animals or machines. These are the tools of initiative; the initiative responsible individuals need in order to participate and to construct their lives.*
John Ralston Saul

---

이 챕터는 다음 주제들을 다루고 있습니다:

- 전 세계 교사 휴게실에서 나누는 교육에 대한 좌절과 질문들 (학습 동기, 고차원적 사고, 대인관계 등)
- 시간 소모적인 수업 계획, 학부모 회의, 교직원 간 오해로 인한 어려움
- 늘어나는 학생 수와 줄어드는 자원 속에서 교육자들이 받는 압박
- 교실, 직원 회의, 학교 위원회, 일대일 대화 등 모든 상황에서 명확하고 심층적인 사고를 돕는 프로세스의 필요성
- 학생, 학부모, 교사 간 유익한 학습으로 이어지는 상호작용 촉진의 필요성

*페이지 2*

## 페이지 16 (SKU-000671170_11_00016.jpeg)

# The Importance of Focused Conversation for Schools — Page 3

The author introduces the "Focused Conversation method" (also called the "art form method", "discussion method", or "O-R-I-D"), developed over 30 years of teaching experience since 1970. The method moves through **four levels of thinking**:

- **Objective:** dealing with data and sensory observation
- **Reflective:** related to personal reactions and associations
- **Interpretive:** about meaning, significance and implications
- **Decisional:** concerned with resolution

The method was developed through four decades of work by the **Institute of Cultural Affairs**, based on research and observations of how people process information.

The book aims to share this method with teachers, administrators, parents, and students. Its use has the potential to:

> a) extend thinking and learning capacity,
> b) make learning meaningful,
> c) make communication in groups effective,
> d) help prevent and solve problems, and
> e) strengthen the effectiveness of evaluation.

## A. Extending Thinking and Learning Capacity

### Facilitating the Capacity to Think Abstractly

The author describes how asking children "What is the story about?" after reading typically yielded superficial answers. The section then introduces an example of using the Focused Conversation method with a preschool group reciting "Little Miss Muffet," beginning with **Objective Questions** such as asking which words were unfamiliar.

---

## 페이지 17 (SKU-000671170_11_00017.jpeg)

# THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS (p. 4)

"Who were the characters?" (Little Miss Muffet, the spider)
"What happened first? Then...Then...?"
"What did Miss Muffet do when she was frightened?"

*Reflective Questions*
"Where have you experienced something like this?" (One child said that his mother made him eat cottage cheese, and he hated it. Several children had stories of scary surprises, and their reactions.)

*Interpretive Questions*
Then I asked, "What is this story all about?"
One little girl thought for a second, then her eyes lit up. "This is about...when you get scared, you can decide if you're going to run away, or not!"

*Decisional Questions*
She finished up with "Next time I will decide by myself what to do!"

[본문 내용 요약: 저자는 어린 아이가 동요의 표면적 의미를 넘어 자신의 삶과 연관된 깊은 의미를 발견한 것에 감탄하며, Focused Conversation 방법이 단계적 사고 과정을 통해 추상적 사고 능력을 높여준다고 설명함]

## Teaching Clear Thinking

[본문 내용 요약: 7학년 교사가 Focused Conversation 방법의 4단계를 교실에 게시하고, 독서 감상문·협동학습·수업 마무리 반성·일기 쓰기 등 다양한 활동에 반복 적용함. 결과적으로 학생들은 더 사려 깊은 글쓰기와 고차원적 사고를 발휘하게 됨]

---

## 페이지 18 (SKU-000671170_11_00018.jpeg)

# The Importance of Focused Conversation for Schools — 5

ing in real life. The conversation method provides a powerful tool for clear thinking and its expression.

## A Logical Structure for Presentations

A high school teacher explains the safety rules for shop class.

*Objective Level:* He describes an accident he has seen using power tools,
*Reflective Level:* He emphasizes what worries him.
*Interpretive Level:* He says a few sentences about what behavior caused the accident.
*Decisional Level:* He then puts out the safety rules he has drawn up and will insist be demonstrated in class.

Chances are high that the class will understand the thinking behind the rules, reach a similar interpretation, and follow the rules.

Not everything can be taught with open-ended questions. Often there is information and wisdom that a teacher has to share with students. The logical structure of the four-level process can be used to make a presentation, giving facts first, then associations and feelings, then the significance or meaning of this information, then conclusions, just as the shop teacher did in the illustration. This provides a window into the thought process of the teacher, communicating clearly how the conclusions are reached. This makes it possible for students to have a more complete understanding of the presentation.

## B. Making Learning Meaningful

### A Structure for Lesson Planning which Includes Application

A fifth grade science teacher provides students with materials and a period of time during class to experiment with building bridges in small groups, then some weights to test the strength of the constructions.

*Objective Level:* First they draw and graph their results.
*Reflective Level:* In the next part of the lesson, the students reflect on what they did and its effects.
*Interpretive Level:* In the third part, students compare their bridge designs with photos or architectural drawings of real bridges and structures to extract the effective design principles.
*Decisional Level:* Finally, students construct a small climbing structure for the playground.

The four steps provide a structure for lesson planning, allowing the presentation of information to be processed and applied before the end of the lesson. As students act upon their learnings, they see the lesson's relevance to real life. Generally, they are motivated to continue learning.

---

## 페이지 19 (SKU-000671170_11_00019.jpeg)

# 6 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

## Dealing with Missed Information and Strengthening Communication

A chemistry teacher begins class with a quick but fascinating demonstration. Several chemicals are mixed together and the series of reactions ends in a cloud of orange smoke. The teacher asks the following questions.

*Objective Level:* "What just happened?" Some students have noticed some things, some others. Together they reconstruct the events in the order in which they happened.

*Reflective Level:* "Where have you seen something like this before?" One student, usually sleepy, tells a dramatic story of an explosion he caused with a chemistry set as a child. Everyone laughs. Another tells a story from a documentary on movie special effects.

*Interpretive Level:* "Why do you think this happened? What are possible explanations? Which explanation makes most sense to you? What do you think is the underlying principle here? How does this compare with the textbook explanation?" Students try out theories, discuss them animatedly, then compare them with the events that happened and their text.

*Decisional Level:* "How might this principle be used in industry?" This provokes some creative applications and provides a spin-off to the next lesson.

As a process for discussions, using the Focused Conversation method strengthens communication. It deals with missed understandings as they arise, whether with students, with parents, or with staff. A simple recall question, "What steps did we just do?" allows students to be filled in on things they missed, without embarrassment. A structured conversation allows more students to participate, as the different levels draw out different student strengths and learning capacities. One student may have vivid associations or strong reactions, while another is fascinated with moral implications. As arguing is replaced with respectful listening to the insights of others, less assertive students find it easier to communicate their ideas publicly.

## C. Making Communication in Meetings and Group Work Effective

### Building Shared Understanding and Respect

At a school in rural Australia, there was much grumbling by the white teachers about the behavior of Aboriginal kids. When the teachers and some parents sat down together to discuss the problem, they started with observations.

*Objective Level:* A teacher said, "When you speak to a child, he doesn't look at you, even when you ask him to."

*Reflective Level:* Then parents and teachers shared their reactions. Teachers felt rebuffed when children didn't look at them, and often grew angry. Parents

---

## 페이지 20 (SKU-000671170_11_00020.jpeg)

# The Importance of Focused Conversation for Schools 7

responded that they expected this behavior from the children, and were baffled by the teachers' anger.

*Interpretive Level:* As they explored the patterns behind these reactions, the teachers discovered that in Aboriginal culture, it is a sign of respect to look down when an elder person is speaking to you, because to look into someone's eyes is to violate their privacy. Parents discovered that people of European descent looked into each other's eyes to demonstrate attention, and experienced looking away as disrespect.

*Decisional Level:* The next step was to act upon ways to bridge the cultural gap. The misunderstandings were resolved in a respectful way that allowed further dialogue.

As people move together step by step to build shared understanding at a deeper and deeper level, respect and consensus begin to emerge. In this way, problems and hot issues can be solved more effectively and in a shorter period of time. The conversation method provides the structure for a group to think clearly together.

## D. Preventing and Solving Problems and Conflict

Two girls were fighting on the playground one day. One of their classmates, trained in peer mediation, intervened.

*Objective Level:* "Mary, what happened here?" "She hit me." "Now Suzanne, you tell me what happened." "I hit her because she pulled my hair."

*Reflective Level:* "How did you feel when this happened?" "It made me really mad." "How do you think she felt?" "She was just as mad as me."

*Interpretive Level:* "Why do you think this fight happened?" "Because we both wanted the swing."

*Decisional Level:* "What could we do to solve this?" "I'll let her go for a while if she gives it to me next."

The use of the conversation method allows people to articulate their reactions, hear others' reactions, and use them as information as they build a larger understanding of the problem. This allows them to solve their problem. Participants do the work of creating their own solutions.

## E. Evaluating and Taking Responsibility for Learning

A high school math teacher on interview night, facing an 18-year-old student who was not doing well and his mother, asked the student a few questions.

---

## 페이지 21 (SKU-000671170_11_00021.jpeg)

# 8 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

*Objective Level:* "Tom, here are the marks I have for you, the ones that are missing, and your current overall mark. What questions do you want to ask me about this data?

*Reflective Level:* What parts of this picture are you most satisfied with? What parts of this concern either of you?

*Interpretive Level:* Where do you discern gaps? What do you think are the underlying problems? How is this class important to you?

*Decisional Level:* What can you do about this? What help do you need from me or from your parents? Here are the times I'm available to help when you need it. What times would be best for you?"

Used in this way, the conversation method can facilitate a developing partnership between the student, teacher, and parent in the student's learning, with the student put into the position of taking responsibility for the decisions.

**Chapter 8. Preventing and Solving Problems**

These conversations demonstrate how students, teachers, administrators, and parents can use the conversation method to resolve misunderstandings and conflicts and resolve difficult problems, in one-on-one and group situations.

The examples in this chapter provide conversations for self-evaluation, as well as evaluating performance and accomplishments of both students and staff in an effective and fair manner that allows for growth.

In this chapter are some specific examples of creative applications of the four-level process. There are sets of conversations that work together to enhance learning, and other unique, creative applications of the four steps of the process.

## 페이지 23 (SKU-000671170_11_00023.jpeg)

# Chapter 2

## The Structure of the Focused Conversation Method

*Of course, intellectual learning includes the amassing and retention of information. But information is an undigested burden unless it is understood… And understanding, comprehension, means that the various parts of the information acquired are grasped in their relations to one another, — a result that is attained only when acquisition is accompanied by constant reflection upon the meaning of what is studied.*
John Dewey

### A Natural Method of Processing Life Experience

The Focused Conversation method analyzed in this chapter is not a new invention. The four stages simply flow from a natural internal process of perception, response, judgement, and decision.

Imagine a toddler learning about the world. She sees a red burner on a stove. She touches it. The pain makes her jump back and howl. As she sticks her fingers in her mouth, she eyes the burner and thinks to herself, "That hurts!" She decides to explore something else. The next time she sees a red burner, she remembers the pain of the first experience, connects the knowledge that a red burner will hurt you, and decides not to touch it (or perhaps to touch it gingerly, to test her new knowledge).

*10*

## 페이지 24 (SKU-000671170_11_00024.jpeg)

# The Structure of the Focused Conversation Method — Page 11

## Main Content

이 페이지는 **집중 대화 방법(Focused Conversation Method)**의 구조와 근원을 설명합니다.

### 학습의 4단계

각 학습자는 다음을 경험합니다:
- **외부 세계 접촉** (객관적 수준 / objective level)
- **내적 연상** (반성적 수준 / reflective level)
- **의미 발견** (해석적 수준 / interpretive level)
- **결론/결정** (결정적 수준 / decisional level)

### 핵심 개념

- 이 4단계는 인간 정신의 자연적 과정을 묘사하며, 대부분 무의식적으로 작동
- 집중 대화 방법은 개인 성찰에서 그룹의 공유된 통찰로 활동 범위를 이동시킴

---

## Roots of the Method (방법의 뿌리)

- Jean Paul Sartre, Edmund Husserl, Søren Kierkegaard의 연구에 기반
- Edgar Schein의 *Process Consultation* 인용
- 신경계는 데이터 수집, 감정 처리, 의미 창조, 결정/실행 시스템으로 구성됨

---

## 페이지 25 (SKU-000671170_11_00025.jpeg)

# THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS (p. 12)

## How We Encounter Life

이 방법은 우리가 어떻게 삶을 만나고 정보를 처리하는지에 대한 가정들을 기반으로 하며, 여러 문화권 사람들의 관찰로부터 도출됩니다.

- **첫째**: 우리는 감각적, 경험적 현실에서 삶의 실재를 발견함 (보고, 냄새 맡고, 만지고, 맛보고, 듣는 것)
- **둘째**: 진정한 감정과 연상은 경험적 체험에서 발생하며, 이 내부 데이터는 외부 관찰 데이터만큼 실재함. Daniel Goleman의 *Emotional Intelligence* 언급
- **셋째**: 우리는 일상적 만남에서 의미를 창조하며, 이는 지속적인 처리 과정을 통해 이루어짐
- **넷째**: 삶에 대한 통찰의 처리는 미래로의 투영을 포함함. 학습의 적용이 처리의 마지막 단계임

## A Whole-System Process

4단계 사고는 총체적 과정으로, 신체의 모든 자원(감각, 기억, 감정, 좌뇌·우뇌, 직관, 의지)을 활용함. 집중 대화는 **전체 시스템 도구**임

## How the Stages Interact

네 가지 관계/단계는 다이어그램으로 묘사됨.

> **[도해 요약]**: 4개의 수직 막대와 화살표로 구성된 이미지 — 왼쪽에서 오른쪽으로 구축되는 애니메이션 영상처럼, 각 단계가 이전 데이터를 기반으로 쌓임을 표현. 세계를 고립된 객체가 아닌 **관계의 묶음**으로 보는 포스트모던적 이해를 반영

---

## 페이지 26 (SKU-000671170_11_00026.jpeg)

# The Structure of the Focused Conversation Method — Page 13

이 페이지는 **Focused Conversation Method(집중 대화 방법)**의 구조를 설명하는 내용으로, 주요 내용을 아래와 같이 요약합니다.

---

## 핵심 내용 요약

### 방법 적용: 효과적인 대화 구조화

집중 대화는 **4단계 질문 수준**을 사용합니다:

- **객관적 수준(Objective level)**: 사실, 외부 현실, 인상에 관한 질문
- **반성적 수준(Reflective level)**: 즉각적인 개인 반응, 감정, 느낌, 연상에 관한 질문
- **해석적 수준(Interpretive level)**: 의미, 가치, 중요성, 시사점 도출을 위한 질문
- **결정적 수준(Decisional level)**: 해결책 도출, 대화 마무리, 미래 결정을 위한 질문

---

### 객관적 수준(The Objective Level)

- **Objective(객관적)**란 마음 외부에 있는 것, 감정이나 의견 없이 사실을 다루는 것
- "D.O.D." = **Directly Observable Data(직접 관찰 가능한 데이터)**
- 객관적 수준 없이는 그룹이 같은 주제를 이야기하는지 확인 불가

---

## 페이지 27 (SKU-000671170_11_00027.jpeg)

# 14 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

The first questions of the conversation get out the facts. They are usually sensory questions about what you see, hear, touch, smell, or taste. Depending on the topic, some senses are more relevant than others. Objective questions ground discussions in shared, observable data rather than assumptions.

Because objective questions are easy to answer, facilitators of over-eager or over-sophisticated groups may be tempted to downplay or omit them. However, each person's unique perception is important for the group to see the whole picture.

In a learning process, clarifying concrete, observable data grounds the learner in reality.

## THE OBJECTIVE LEVEL IN A NUTSHELL

| 항목 | 내용 |
|---|---|
| **Focus of the questions** | Data, the "facts" about the topic, external reality |
| **What it does for the group** | Ensures everyone deals with the same body of data |
| **Questions are in relation to** | The senses: what is seen, heard, touched, etc. |
| **Key questions** | What objects do you see? What words or phrases stand out? What happened? |
| **Traps and pitfalls** | Asking closed questions; ignoring objective questions as "too trivial" |
| **If this level is omitted** | No shared observation; various comments may seem unrelated |

## The Reflective Level

Next comes the reflective stage in which participants examine their personal responses to the topic — feelings, moods, memories, and associations. Both positive and negative reactions provide important information about a topic. This kind of internal data is just as real and important as objective data. If

---

## 페이지 28 (SKU-000671170_11_00028.jpeg)

# The Structure of the Focused Conversation Method — Page 15

이 페이지는 **Focused Conversation Method**의 **반성적(Reflective) 수준**에 대해 설명하는 내용입니다.

## 주요 내용 요약

**반성적 질문의 역할:**
- 참여자들이 첫 번째 수준(객관적 데이터)에서 인식한 정보를 능동적으로 되돌아보게 함
- 예: "이것과 관련된 경험이 있나요?", "무엇이 놀라웠나요?", "무엇이 당신을 불편하게 했나요?"

**반성적 질문이 없을 때의 문제:**
- 숨겨진 이미지, 연상, 감정들이 공유되지 않음
- 직관, 기억, 감정, 상상력이 결코 환기되지 않음
- 참여자들이 정보와 자신의 삶의 연관성을 파악하지 못함

**반성적 수준의 효과:**
- 외부 데이터와 내부 반응 사이의 반복적 순환에서 벗어나게 함
- 학습과 동기 부여를 위한 강력한 도구
- 학생들이 이야기 속 인물을 자신의 삶과 연결 지을 수 있도록 도움

> Susan Langer의 저서 *Problems of Art*에서 차용한 개념: 이 방법은 "주관적인 것을 외부화하고 객관화"함으로써 평소 인식하지 못했던 감정과 통찰을 표면으로 끌어냄.

---

## 페이지 29 (SKU-000671170_11_00029.jpeg)

# 16 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

## THE REFLECTIVE LEVEL IN A NUTSHELL

| 항목 | 내용 |
|------|------|
| **Focus of the questions** | Internal relationship to the data |
| **What it does for the group** | Reveals individuals' initial responses and validates their experience |
| **Questions are in relation to** | Associations, memories, feelings, moods, or emotional tones |
| **Key questions** | What does it remind you of? Which part surprised you? What delighted you? Where did you struggle? |
| **Traps and pitfalls** | Limiting the discussion to an either/or survey of likes and dislikes; Asking vague or broad questions that don't evoke relevant personal associations; Asking questions that demand embarrassingly personal answers |
| **If this level is omitted** | The world of intuition, memory, emotion, and imagination is ignored, and no shared personal experience is articulated on which to build meaning |

## The Interpretive Level

The third area of questioning is the interpretive level where the real grappling is done with the meaning of a topic. The interpretive responses build on objective data, and the associations or feelings from the reflective level. Interpretive questions highlight the layers of meaning and purpose that people ascribe to situations and responses. They invite a group to create the significance or importance of an occasion. A clue word at this level is "why". Interpretive questions help people build a "story" of what is happening. The question of values or ethics may appear, as in "What values does this reveal?"

This level is extremely important for learning, since there is an emphasis on meaningful connections, integration, understanding, and comprehension. Once the information is acknowledged and previous experience with it is made conscious, critical thinking, analysis and synthesis can happen. With questions such as "What is this story about?" or "How might the chemical reaction of soda and an acid apply to baking a cake?", students can make astounding connections with patterns that relate to their own life experience.

## THE INTERPRETIVE LEVEL IN A NUTSHELL

| 항목 | 내용 |
|------|------|
| **Focus of the questions** | The life meaning of the topic |
| **What it does for the group** | Draws out the significance from the data for the group; focuses on learnings |
| **Questions are in relation to** | Layers of meaning, purpose, significance, implications, "story", values, patterns. Considering alternatives, options. Comprehension |
| **Key questions** | Why is this happening? What is this all about? How does this compare? What does all this mean for us? How will this affect our work? What are we learning from this? What is the larger pattern emerging? What is the insight? |
| **Traps and pitfalls** | Inserting pre-cooked meaning that prevents real insight; over-intellectualizing or over-abstracting; judging responses as right or wrong |
| **If this level is omitted** | Group gets no chance to make sense out of the first two levels. No higher-order thinking goes into decision-making |

---

## 페이지 30 (SKU-000671170_11_00030.jpeg)

# The Structure of the Focused Conversation Method (p. 17)

## The Decisional Level

The fourth part of the focused conversation is the **decisional level** where implications and new directions are discussed. Questions allow people to take data from previous levels and use it to make choices.

Some decisional questions allow people to name their own relationships to their situation. Others may call for short or long-term decisions — written decisions or actions. Class exercises or homework assignments may extend or apply the learnings of the lesson.

> Without some decision, the conversation is largely a waste of time. This is the place where application of knowledge to the everyday world is explored.

---

## 표: THE DECISIONAL LEVEL IN A NUTSHELL

| 항목 | 내용 |
|---|---|
| **Focus of the questions** | Resolution, implications, application, new directions |
| **What it does for the group** | Makes the conversation relevant for the future |
| **Questions are in relation to** | Consensus, implementation, action, summarizing, application of knowledge, future directions |
| **Key questions** | How might you use this? How would you summarize your learning? What decision is called for? What are your next steps? |
| **Traps and pitfalls** | Forcing a decision when group is not ready or avoiding pushing group for decision |
| **If this level is omitted** | Learning is not consolidated, and responses from the first three levels are not applied or tested in real life |

---

마지막 단락에서는 이 챕터가 교육 연구에 기반한 Focused Conversation 방법을 소개했으며, Chapter 3(연구 맥락)과 Chapter 4(설계 절차)를 참조하도록 안내합니다.

## 페이지 31 (SKU-000671170_11_00031.jpeg)

# Chapter 3

## The Research behind the Method

*The art of remembering is the art of thinking;...when we wish to fix a new thing in either our own mind or a pupil's, our conscious effort should not be so much to impress and retain it as to connect it with something else already there. The connecting is the thinking; and if we attend clearly to the connection, the connected thing will certainly be likely to remain within recall.*

William James

---

### The Birth of the Conversation Method

이 챕터는 ICA(Institute of Cultural Affairs)가 1954년경부터 대학생과 성인들을 대상으로 미술 강의 및 주말 세미나에서 **Focused Conversation 방법**을 활용하기 시작했다고 설명합니다.

교수들은 이 방법이 학생들로 하여금 그림이나 영화를 "삶의 단면"으로 활용해 학습 내용을 적용하는 데 도움이 된다는 것을 발견했습니다.

Brian Stanfield의 *The Art of Focused Conversation* 인용을 통해, Picasso의 *Guernica* 그림을 활용한 Joe Mathews 교수의 수업 사례가 소개되며, 학생들에게 그림 속 사물을 묘사하고 내면의 반응을 탐색하도록 유도하는 장면이 나옵니다.

## 페이지 32 (SKU-000671170_11_00032.jpeg)

# The Research behind the Method (19페이지)

## 본문 요약 및 추출

이 페이지는 **Focused Conversation Method(집중 대화 방법)**의 연구적 배경을 설명합니다.

---

### 주요 내용

**예술을 통한 교육 실험**
- 학생들이 예술을 "문화적 사물" 또는 "장식적 대상"으로 보던 시각에서, 삶과 직결된 것으로 인식하게 되는 변화 경험
- 참가자: *"갑자기 이 예술 형태들이 나에게 요구를 하고 있다는 것을 알았다. 깨어나라! 진짜 삶을 살아라."*

**Focused Conversation Method 발전**
- Mathews와 동료들이 여러 수업에서 참여적 성찰 방식을 실험
- **Art Form Conversation**으로 발전 — 학생들이 스스로 이미지를 개발하도록 돕는 도구

**키르케고르(Søren Kierkegaard)의 영향**
- 의식의 과정을 연구하는 데 도움
- 자아를 관찰, 내적 반응, 통찰, 결정의 연속으로 묘사

---

### 박스 인용 (Kierkegaard, *The Sickness Unto Death*)

> "The self is a relation [four vertical bars], which, in relating itself to itself [first arrow], and willing itself to be itself [second arrow], grounds itself transparently in the power that posit it [third arrow]."

---

### 대화 방법의 4단계 구조

| 단계 | 내용 |
|------|------|
| **객관적(Objective)** | 눈앞에 있는 것에 집중 |
| **성찰적(Reflective)** | 내면 이미지 인식 및 표현 |
| **해석적(Interpretive)** | 이미지의 관련성 검토 |
| **결정적(Decisional)** | 삶과 행동에 미치는 영향 선택 |

---

## 페이지 33 (SKU-000671170_11_00033.jpeg)

# 20 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

## The Theory of Imaginal Education

This section describes how ICA (Institute of Cultural Affairs) moved to the Fifth City community in Chicago's West Side and founded a community preschool. The staff developed a theory called **Imaginal Education** to address challenges of quality early childhood education in the inner city.

### Key Research Question
"How do people learn, and how does their behavior change as a result of their learning?"

### Five Basic Tenets of Imaginal Education
1. People operate out of images
2. Images determine behavior
3. Messages that reach a person affect the images he or she has
4. Images can change
5. When images change, behavior changes

### Theoretical Influences
- **Kenneth Boulding** (*The Image*) – role of images in underlie behavior
- **Marshall McLuhan** (*The Medium is the Message*) – images created by media have as much effect as content
- **Paulo Friere** (*Pedagogy of the Oppressed*) – literacy training and inner image of victimization

### Brain and Learning Research
David Perkins (*Smart Schools*) is quoted discussing the connection between understanding and mental images — described as holistic, highly integrated knowledge representations that help us work with topics or subjects.

---

## 페이지 34 (SKU-000671170_11_00034.jpeg)

# The Research behind the Method 21

Karen Olness, interviewed by Bill Moyers in *Healing and the Mind*, observes that when children use their imaginations, there is an impact on physiological responses. She says "my intuition is that whatever energy is associated with the construction of images and the process of thinking transmits a message to a cascade of body processes".

Many of the teachers who had begun the work on imaginal education continued to work together in an education guild. In a section called "Growing through Image Shifting" in one of their early papers they summarized their approach:

> Writers like McLuhan and Boulding have pushed us to realize that everyone has "images" that give form to one's world and determine behavior...

*(단락 계속: 이미지가 학습과 행동에 미치는 영향, 교사와 학생 간의 이미지 상호작용, 학습은 자신과 세계의 이미지를 재구성하는 과정이라는 내용)*

## Using Messages as a Teaching Tool

Imaginal Education, as described by the Institute staff in the *Fifth City Preschool Manual*, is "the process by which messages are intentionally directed to a person's images in order to give the opportunity for a change of image".

The imaginal approach recognizes that the messages we receive from the world interact with the values we hold...

*(단락 계속: 교사가 학생에게 보내는 메시지의 역할, 이미지 변화의 기회 제공에 관한 내용)*

> The imaginal educator creates messages which give a student an opportunity to change his image. However, change finally remains within the decisional realm of the student; the teacher cannot force an image change, but can only send messages. Because a

---

## **Sending Messages with both Structure and Content**

Jerome Bruner의 *The Process of Education* 인용:
> "the structure, not the content, of a discipline is the key to comprehension and retention"

- 학교는 구조와 과정을 통해 메시지를 전달하는 시스템
- 언어적 메시지와 구조적 메시지가 서로 모순될 때 **혼란과 역효과** 발생
- 교사의 무의식적 메시지가 학생에게 강력한 영향을 미침
- 부정적 메시지("He's lazy!")는 학생의 자아상에 깊이 내재화됨
- 반면 긍정적이고 일관된 메시지는 학업 성취를 크게 향상시킬 수 있음

## 페이지 37 (SKU-000671170_11_00037.jpeg)

# 24 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

This page discusses how teachers serve as guides for student learning rather than information transmitters, and introduces the **Focused Conversation method** in relation to learning theories.

---

## Key Sections:

### Main Text
- Teachers act as catalysts in a **trilogue** between information, student, and teacher
- Quote from **OliveAnn Slotta** (*The Image-Based Instruction Workbook*) about shifting from curriculum-driven to inquiry-driven classrooms

---

### Complementary Learning Theories

The Focused Conversation method is compared to several learning frameworks:

| Theorist | Framework | Connection |
|----------|-----------|------------|
| **David Kolb** | *Learning Style Inventory* | Each conversation step evokes different learning styles |
| **Bernice McCarthy** | 4-Mat System | Moves through learning cycle *in sequence* across four modes |
| **Alma Flor Ada** | Bilingual reading program (California) | Uses a closely related four-step process for understanding texts |

---

### Core Idea
The conversation method naturally incorporates all students' preferred learning modes, making it a **natural learning progression** that stretches each student beyond their preferred style.

---

## 페이지 38 (SKU-000671170_11_00038.jpeg)

# The Research behind the Method 25

The Methodology of Creative Education … views the learning process as centering on the learner, who is encouraged to develop critical thinking skills and an attitude of creative response in the face of real-life situations . . . .

In every act of real learning there are four phases:

*Descriptive phase:* In this initial phase the children receive information; that is, they learn what the text says … Such questions are undoubtedly important, but they are not enough. A discussion that stays at this level suggests that reading is a passive, receptive, and in a sense, domesticating process.

*Personal interpretive phase:* Once the information has been presented, the children are invited to weigh it against their own experiences, feelings, and emotions…

*Critical phase:* Once the children have compared and contrasted what is presented in the reading with their personal experiences, they are ready to move on to a critical analysis, to the level of generalized reflection…

*Creative phase:* …The process is completed only when the children can draw on [the awakening of critical awareness] in order to make decisions regarding the world around them….

---

## Creating Curriculum

### Spiral curriculum

Another dimension of imaginal education is the related concept of a "spiral curriculum," which grew out of a theory proposed by Jerome Bruner that said that "Any subject can be taught to any child at any age in some form that is honest." In *The Process of Education*, he elaborated:

> A spiral curriculum (is) the idea that in teaching a subject you begin with an "intuitive" account that is well within the reach of a student, and then circle back later to a more formal or highly structured account…

---

## 페이지 39 (SKU-000671170_11_00039.jpeg)

# 26 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

This page discusses two main topics:

## Comprehensive Curriculum and the Conversation Method

The text explains how teaching builds on what students already know, extending their understanding through focused conversation. It references David Perkins' *Smart Schools*, noting that education should extend students' **capacity** to think clearly and apply learnings, and their **consciousness** to perceive more and make wise decisions.

The conversation method bridges theory and practice through questions at the decisional level, leading to application. The text emphasizes that learning is only verified when a student's behavior changes — applying knowledge in real life.

## Tools to Carry Messages

As research on how images affect behavior developed, teachers created curricula using **imaginal education** to build positive images in students. A "tools chart" was developed — a palette of activities and materials using different media, appealing to all senses, and meeting a wide range of learning styles.

The nine categories of tools included:
- Three **literary media**: drama, poetry, and prose
- Three **rhythmic media**: instruments, dance, and song
- (additional categories mentioned continuing on next page)

---

## 페이지 40 (SKU-000671170_11_00040.jpeg)

# The Research behind the Method 27

three plastic media — architecture, sculpture, and visual art.

This development of comprehensive learning tools has been enriched by Howard Gardner's theory of multiple intelligences in his book, *Frames of Mind*. Gardner says, in essence, that there are multiple forms of intelligence. Everyone has intelligence, some forms of which are weaker and some stronger. We have traditionally measured only verbal-linguistic and mathematical-logical intelligence. However, there are at least six more: visual-spatial intelligence, kinesthetic or bodily intelligence, musical intelligence, intra- and inter-personal intelligences, and finally "natural" intelligence (knowing through nature). We learn content more easily through our stronger intelligences, and we can also strengthen our weaker ones. As any group of students has strengths in different combinations of intelligences, these can be used like an artist's palette to teach content in a way that reaches all the students in ways that they can easily learn. For example, teaching the alphabet by tracing the letters (kinesthetic and visual intelligence), telling a story based on each letter (verbal-linguistic intelligence), and singing the alphabet song (musical intelligence) will go a long way to ensure that everyone in the class learns the letters as easily as possible.

The conversation method can be used in conjunction with a variety of tools and intelligences. Using the conversation method to debrief classroom events deepens the learning experience. Since the method was originally developed to understand art forms, it is ideal for reflecting on visual creations. A highly effective use I have seen is to reflect on trust-building exercises done in a high ropes course with sixth and seventh grade students. In a very different way, the evaluation conversation with preschoolers in Chapter 9 asks questions at each level that require kinesthetic or visual responses as well as verbal ones. David Lazear, in *Teaching through Multiple Intelligences*, shares many examples of the use of the conversation method with curriculum events using different intelligences.

The conversation method takes the experiences that students have in the classroom, or in other situations, and extends their experience to deeper interpretation and application to life. When I was working with a preschool in the village of Bayad, in rural Egypt in 1977, the students in the preschool had an image of the world around them that was limited to immediate experience of their village. I had only a vague image of ancient Egypt, and that image had no connection with the present day. The preschool staff and I took the students on a field trip one day, walking our charges down to the river and crossing on a felucca, or sailboat, to the government building on the other side of the Nile in the provincial capital of Beni Suef. In the lobby of the government building was a large mural. As we stood in front of it, we used the conversation method to understand what was there. First we noted the step pyramid of Maidum on the far right, then farmers with hand tools and buffalo-drawn plows to the left of that, then a tractor, then a factory and city images on the left. As we recognized the right-to-left progression of the images and

---

→ **Focused Conversation 방법**을 통해 이 5가지 요소를 수업에 통합 가능

## 페이지 42 (SKU-000671170_11_00042.jpeg)

# The Research behind the Method — Page 29

## Main Text

This page continues a discussion about experiential/imaginal teaching methods, covering two main ideas:

**Classroom Example — Australian Settlement Lesson:**
- Teacher had students imaginatively experience being convicts transported to Australia
- Students lay on carpet, imagined being packed into convict ships, seasick, with little food
- Upon "arriving," they imagined finding food, building shelter in an unfamiliar land
- Followed by focused discussion drawing out feelings and imagination
- Result: Students gained physical/emotional sense of early white Australian settlement history
- Even typically disruptive boys were attentive and creative in participation

**Broader Vision of Imaginal Teaching:**
- Author reflects on what society would be like if students left school with capacity to:
  - Observe events around them
  - Connect new information with previous experiences
  - Interpret meaning and impact of experiences
  - Act on their insights
- Quote from psychologist **Jean Houston** (New York lecture, 1987):
  > "We're living in the attic of ourselves. We don't use the first three floors, and the basement is locked, until it wells up in an explosion."
- Imaginal teaching provides tools to unlock fuller consciousness
- Described as "an awesome vision"

## 페이지 45 (SKU-000671170_11_00045.jpeg)

# Chapter 4

## Preparing a Conversation

*Curriculum must be connected with and relevant to the lives of students. Without this connection, students see no value in their learning and are not motivated to continue learning. Curriculum becomes useful only when it is relevant and meaningful to the students.*
— R. Bruce Williams

---

이 챕터는 **Focused Conversation 방법**이 모든 상황에 적합한 도구인지 점검하는 내용으로 시작합니다.

### 핵심 내용 요약:

- Focused Conversation은 **모든 것을 가르치거나 모든 회의를 관리하는 데** 사용할 수 없음
- 학생들에게 사실을 단순 전달하거나 설득하는 데는 적합하지 않음
- **사용 적합 상황**: 그룹이 주제의 함의를 탐구하고 스스로 결론에 도달하길 원할 때
- 단순한 사실 전달이 목적이라면 **강의, 시연, 메모** 등이 더 적합
- 단, 정보 전달 후 **처리 및 결론 도출**을 위해 대화가 유용할 수 있음
- Chapter 10에 4단계 형식을 구조로 활용한 프레젠테이션 예시 수록

## 페이지 46 (SKU-000671170_11_00046.jpeg)

# Preparing a Conversation 33

## Preparatory Steps

This section explains that quality conversation begins with quality preparation, and walks through the steps of preparing a quality conversation.

Key points covered:
- Pre-written conversations in the book can be adapted to specific situations
- A blackline worksheet in the appendix guides preparation
- Using pencil and sticky notes is recommended

### 1. Focusing the Topic

The section discusses how determining the topic requires:
- Picking a concrete target topic and starting point
- Avoiding abstract/general levels that obscure meaningful dialogue
- A focused topic helps the group articulate objective data and probe at the interpretive level

**Examples given:**
- Social studies curriculum discussing "politics" → could be focused on a political rally experience, recent news events, or a textbook chapter on political parties
- A parent group considering "student safety" → more focused topics might be "playground safety," "bus safety," or "safety while crossing..."

---

## 페이지 47 (SKU-000671170_11_00047.jpeg)

# THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS (p. 34)

## 주요 내용 요약

이 페이지는 대화 설계를 위한 단계별 지침을 다루고 있습니다:

---

### 2. 대화의 의도 작성 (Write Down the Intent of the Conversation)

- **Rational aim**: 대화의 실질적/구체적 학습 목표 (내용 결과물)
- **Experiential aim**: 과정을 통해 그룹에 전달되는 내적 변화나 이미지 (과정 결과물)
- 목표를 미리 명확히 하여 대화를 설계할 것을 권고
- 준비 과정에서 목표를 수정·보완할 것을 권장

---

### 3. 객관적 질문의 구체적 출발점 확보 (Ensure a Concrete Beginning Point for Your Objective Questions)

- 대화는 관찰 가능한 구체적 대상이나 공유 경험에 관한 객관적 질문으로 시작
- 추상적인 것에서 시작하면 대화가 혼란스러워짐
- "용어를 정의하라"고 요청하는 방식으로 시작하면 해석 단계로 바로 넘어가는 문제 발생
- 구체적인 것에서 시작하는 토론이 더 효과적

---

## 페이지 48 (SKU-000671170_11_00048.jpeg)

# Preparing a Conversation 35

이 페이지는 Focused Conversation 방법론에 관한 책의 35페이지로, 대화 준비 방법을 설명합니다.

## 주요 내용 요약

**구체적 경험에서 시작하기**
- 인종차별, 운동장 안전 등 추상적 주제도 구체적인 출발점(뉴스 기사, 단편 소설, 실제 사건 등)에서 시작해야 효과적임
- 구체적 현실에서 시작할 때 더 솔직하고 사려 깊은 반응이 나옴

## 4. 합리적·경험적 목표를 위한 질문 브레인스토밍

- 주제와 목표를 검토한 후 자유롭게 모든 가능한 질문을 작성
- 순서 없이 떠오르는 대로 종이나 포스트잇에 기록

---

## 📋 다양한 연령/성숙도/학년에 맞는 질문 적용

- 어린 학생: 여러 개의 **객관적 질문**이 효과적 (누가, 무슨 일이, 순서 등)
- 나이 든 학생: 객관적 질문을 건너뛰는 경향이 있으나 권장하지 않음
- 어린 학생은 해석적 수준에 어려움을 가질 수 있으므로 **가능한 답변 예시 제공** 권장
- 성숙한 학생은 더 깊은 해석적 질문 필요
- 4단계 질문 모두 포함 시 다양한 사고 수준의 학생 모두 참여 가능

---

## 페이지 49 (SKU-000671170_11_00049.jpeg)

# 36 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

이 페이지는 책의 본문으로, 주요 내용을 아래와 같이 요약합니다:

## 5. Select and Order the Questions You Will Ask

- 작성한 모든 질문이 필요하지 않으며, 목표 달성을 위한 핵심 질문을 선별
- 선택한 질문들을 워크시트 칼럼에 배치하고, 각 레벨 내에서 흐름이 자연스럽도록 순서를 재배열

### How Many Questions at Each Level?

각 레벨에서 필요한 질문 수에 대한 안내:
- **객관적 레벨**: 충분한 데이터 샘플링을 위해 최소 4개 질문 필요
- **반성적 레벨**: 최소 2개 이상의 반성적 질문 필요
- **해석적 레벨**: 구체적 사례나 의견을 이끌어내기 위한 추가 질문 필요
- **결정적 레벨**: 2~3개의 질문이 유용 (결정 사항, 다음 단계, 담당자 등)

## 6. Rehearse the Conversation in Your Head

- 각 질문을 스스로 경험해보며 참가자 관점에서 질문의 흐름과 자연스러움을 확인

---

## 페이지 50 (SKU-000671170_11_00050.jpeg)

# Preparing a Conversation 37

이 페이지는 대화 준비 방법에 관한 책의 37페이지로, **7. Prepare Your Opening Carefully** 섹션을 포함합니다.

## 주요 내용 요약

**도입부**: 대화 흐름을 연습하면 질문들이 자연스럽게 이어지는 하나의 seamless한 대화가 된다고 설명.

## 7. 오프닝을 신중하게 준비하라

오프닝 코멘트는 다음 기능들을 수행:

- **Invitation(초대)**: 그룹이 대화에 참여하도록 초대
- **Focus(초점)**: 대화의 주제와 목적 명시
- **Context(맥락)**: 대화가 왜 지금 필요한지 배경 설명, 소요 시간 안내
- **Forestall any objections(반론 차단)**: 대화를 피하려는 이유를 미리 처리
- **Focus on the concrete beginning point(구체적 시작점 집중)**: 참가자들의 주의를 선택한 시작점으로 유도
- **Set ground rules(기본 규칙 설정)**: 행동 기대치를 명확히 제시하여 안전한 참여 환경 조성

---

## 페이지 51 (SKU-000671170_11_00051.jpeg)

# 38 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

이 페이지는 책의 38페이지로, 대화 준비와 진행에 관한 지침을 담고 있습니다.

## 주요 섹션 요약

**8. Prepare the Closing Carefully**
- 대화를 마무리할 때 사용할 말을 미리 작성해 둘 것
- 해결되지 않은 문제들도 인정하고, 다음 수업을 위해 메모해 둘 것
- 그룹의 통찰을 확인하고, 결과 활용 방법을 언급할 것

**9. Reflect on the Conversation, the Group, and Yourself**
- 전체 대화를 흐름에 따라 검토할 것
- 질문 간의 자연스러운 연결 확인
- 타임라인을 준비하고, 자신의 편견·강점·약점을 성찰할 것

**10. Learning from Your Experience**
- 대화를 이끈 후, 실제로 어떻게 진행되었는지 되돌아볼 것
- 수정할 사항을 메모하여 미래 대화 준비에 활용할 것

---

## 페이지 52 (SKU-000671170_11_00052.jpeg)

# Chapter 5

## Leading a Focused Conversation

*There are ample opportunities to orient instruction toward higher levels of understanding, introduce and exercise languages of thinking, cultivate intellectual passions, seek out integrative mental images, foster learning to learn, and teach for transfer. The smart school makes the most of these opportunities.*
David Perkins

This chapter opens by addressing the question of why focused conversations need to be led, noting that while the Focused Conversation method follows a natural thinking pattern, systematic thinking requires discipline. The text explains that a skilled facilitator can bring focus and creativity to group discussions, and that even well-designed conversations can face challenges — such as some participants dominating while others stay silent, or conversations drifting off course. Conversation leaders help groups avoid common pitfalls like trivia, argumentation, and abstraction.

---

*Page 39*

---

## 페이지 53 (SKU-000671170_11_00053.jpeg)

# 40 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

## How to Lead a Focused Conversation

### Facilitator's Responsibility

이 페이지는 집중 대화(Focused Conversation)를 이끄는 방법에 대해 설명합니다.

**주요 내용 요약:**

- **진행자의 책임**: 그룹이 스스로 최선의 사고를 할 수 있도록 안내하고, 참가자들의 경험에 영향을 미치는 과정의 메시지에 책임을 짐

- **1. 적절한 환경 설정 (Set a Suitable Environment)**: 모든 참가자가 서로 보고 들을 수 있는 공간 배치 권장 (반원형 또는 직사각형 배치)

- **2. 초대 (Invitation)**: 그룹이 자리를 잡고 안정될 수 있도록 초대

- **3. 시작 (Opening)**: 대화의 목적, 주제, 역사적 중요성, 결과 활용 방법 등에 대한 계획된 시작 발언으로 출발

---

## 페이지 54 (SKU-000671170_11_00054.jpeg)

# Leading a Focused Conversation 41

이 페이지는 "Leading a Focused Conversation"이라는 책의 41페이지로, 집중 대화를 이끄는 방법에 대한 내용을 담고 있습니다.

## 주요 내용 요약

### 4. The First Questions (첫 번째 질문)
- 참가자들이 답하기 쉬운 **객관적인 질문**으로 시작하여 분위기를 풀 것을 권장
- 15명 이상일 경우 진행 방식 조정 권고
- 응답은 짧고 자연스럽게 이어지도록 유도
- "틀린 답은 없다"는 분위기 조성의 중요성 강조
- 객관적 질문 답변이 가장 쉽고도 어려운 부분이 될 수 있음

### 5. Subsequent Questions (후속 질문)
- 두 번째 질문부터는 **전체 그룹**을 대상으로 질문
- 순서 없이 누구든 자유롭게 답할 수 있도록 안내

> 핵심: 탐구와 존중의 문화를 형성하면 대부분의 참가자들이 안전하게 참여할 수 있는 환경이 만들어진다.

---

## 페이지 55 (SKU-000671170_11_00055.jpeg)

# 42 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

## 6. Getting off the Topic: What Do You Do?

이 섹션은 대화 참가자들이 주제를 벗어났을 때 대처 방법을 설명합니다. 핵심 내용: 주제 이탈은 규율 문제가 아니며, 인간의 연상적 사고 때문에 자연스럽게 발생함. 대화 진행자는 주제를 벗어난 발언을 인정하면서도 그룹의 논의를 요약하고 질문으로 다시 돌아오는 방식으로 대응해야 함.

## 7. What about Wrong Answers?

이 섹션은 사실적·역사적·지리적으로 틀린 답변, 공격적인 발언, 의도적으로 이상한 답변을 하는 경우에 대한 대처 방법을 다룹니다. 핵심 내용:

- **틀린 답변**: 부드럽게 정정하되 학생을 위축시키지 않도록 함
- **공격적 발언**: 발언의 의도와 분위기를 파악하고, 필요시 다른 표현을 요청하거나 기본 규칙을 재확립
- **의도적인 이상한 답변**: 단순히 넘기거나 "왜 그렇게 생각하나요?"라고 물어보는 방식으로 대응하며, 자존감을 높이는 방식으로 처리

---

## 페이지 56 (SKU-000671170_11_00056.jpeg)

# Leading a Focused Conversation 43

이 페이지는 "Leading a Focused Conversation"이라는 책의 43페이지로, 교사/진행자를 위한 대화 진행 방법에 대해 설명합니다.

## 주요 내용 요약

**창의적 사고와 다양한 관점**
- 학생들의 다양한 시각을 수용하면 더 창의적인 사고가 가능하며, 모든 관점을 들을 때 전체적인 그림이 완성됨

**진행자의 객관성 유지**
- 진행자가 자신의 의제를 앞세우면 그룹의 신뢰를 잃음
- 교육적 내용 전달은 대화 전/후에 별도로 할 것을 권장

## 8. 길거나 추상적인 답변: 어떻게 할 것인가?
- 구체적인 질문이 구체적인 답변을 이끌어냄
- 추상적인 답변에는 "더 간단히 말씀해 주시겠어요?" 또는 "구체적인 예를 들어주시겠어요?"라고 요청할 것
- 모호한 진술이 명확해지지 않으면 대화가 더 깊은 통찰을 이끌 수 없음

## 9. 논쟁이 시작되면 어떻게 할 것인가?
- 대화 방법에서는 서로 다른 의견이 있을 수 있으나, 핵심은 찬반 토론이 아닌 응답을 명확히 하고 설명하는 것임

---

## 페이지 57 (SKU-000671170_11_00057.jpeg)

# THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS (p. 44)

## 주요 내용 요약

**토론 진행 지침** (앞부분 계속)
- 선언적 발언에 대해 근거를 물어볼 것
- 모든 관점을 존중하고, 반대 의견도 수용하는 방식으로 진행
- 방해하는 참가자는 다른 사람 발언 후 의견 제시하도록 유도

### 10. Closing (마무리)
- 그룹의 결론을 요약하며 토론 마무리
- 참가자에게 감사 표시
- 메모 활용 방법 공지 및 사본 제공 가능성 언급

---

## Facilitating a Conversation Is Not Teaching Content

**핵심 논점:**
- 교사의 역할은 단순히 전문 지식을 전달하는 것이 아님
- 연구에 따르면 새로운 정보는 학생이 기존 지식과 연결할 때 비로소 습득됨
- 집중 대화(focused conversation)는 학생 스스로 정보를 처리하고 내면화하도록 도움
- 개방형 질문은 학생들의 사고 방식을 변화시킴

**저자의 경험:**
- 시카고 내 학교에서 7~8학년 음악 교사로 첫 부임
- 처음에는 전문가 역할을 맡았으나... (다음 페이지로 이어짐)

---

## 페이지 58 (SKU-000671170_11_00058.jpeg)

# Leading a Focused Conversation 45

이 페이지는 교사의 경험과 촉진적 학습에 관한 내용을 다루고 있습니다. 주요 내용을 요약하면:

## 주요 내용

**교사의 경험담**
- 학생들에게 필요한 것에 집중한 Black 음악 역사 수업 개설
- 학생들이 음악뿐 아니라 자신의 지혜와 경험의 중요성을 배움
- "답을 알아야 한다"는 부담을 내려놓고 학습 촉진자 역할 수행

**교사의 두 가지 역할 구분**
- 정보를 제시하는 역할
- 정보 처리를 촉진하는 역할 (학생의 지식에 대한 갈망 생성)

**John Kloepfer 인용** (*The Art of Formative Questioning*)
- 촉진자의 핵심 자질: **docta ignorantia** (소크라테스적 열린 무지)
- 진정한 열린 마음 없이는 진정한 통찰 불가

**개입 습관의 문제**
- 교사들은 수정·보완하도록 훈련됨
- 교사가 답을 유도하면 학생들은 참여를 꺼리게 됨

**두 학생 사례 (Hamlet 수업)**
- 외향적 학생 vs 내향적 학생의 상반된 반응 소개

---

## 페이지 59 (SKU-000671170_11_00059.jpeg)

# 46 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

이 페이지는 교사와 학생 간의 대화 촉진에 관한 내용을 담고 있습니다. 주요 내용을 요약하면:

## 핵심 주제들

**교사 조종에 대한 학생 반응**
- 학생들은 교사가 특정 답변으로 유도하려 한다고 느낄 때 분노함
- 교육자들도 마찬가지로 조종당한다고 느끼면 강하게 반발함

**대화 촉진자의 준비 자세**
- 진정한 호기심 개발이 필요
- "올바른 답"을 기대하는 것과 반대되는 태도 요구
- 그룹이 어떤 새로운 통찰을 가져올지 궁금해하는 자세

**발표와 대화 촉진의 결합**
- 발표와 성찰이 결합될 때 두 역할 분리가 중요
- "발표자" 모자와 "촉진자" 모자를 구분하는 것이 효과적
- 플립차트나 칠판에 질문을 적으면 사람과 의견을 분리하는 데 도움

**모든 정답을 포기하는 것의 어려움**
- 교사에게는 혼란스럽거나 두려운 일일 수 있음

## 페이지 60 (SKU-000671170_11_00060.jpeg)

# Leading a Focused Conversation 47

## Why My Conversation Didn't Work and What To Do About It

이 페이지는 다음 내용을 다루고 있습니다:

- 교사로서의 역할을 유지하면서 학생들로부터 존중을 얻는 방법에 대한 통찰
- 그룹 토론을 통해 학생들이 집단적으로 문제를 해결할 수 있다는 점
- 위의 지침들이 학습과 사려 깊은 의사결정을 촉진하는 데 도움이 된다는 설명

**"왜 내 대화가 잘 되지 않았는가, 그리고 어떻게 해야 하는가"** 섹션에서는:

- 좋은 방법, 충분한 준비, 참여 의지가 있어도 모든 대화가 잘 되지는 않는다는 점
- 어려운 경험을 "내가 할 수 없는 것"으로 치부하지 말고 성찰하는 것이 중요하다는 점
- 이어지는 표(다음 페이지)는 대화에서 잘못될 수 있는 것과 대처 전략을 정리한 내용임을 예고

> 표의 내용은 교실과 교직원/학부모 회의 모두에 적용 가능하며, 모든 소통 문제를 다루지는 않음을 명시

---

## 페이지 61 (SKU-000671170_11_00061.jpeg)

# 48 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

## 자주 보고되는 문제들과 해결책 표

**표 구성: 자주 보고되는 문제 | 가능한 원인 | 가능한 해결책**

---

### 1. Group Isn't focusing (그룹이 집중하지 못함)

- **원인:** 혼란스럽거나 불친절한 환경 → 조용한 공간 재배치, 참가자들이 서로 볼 수 있도록 착석
- **원인:** 그룹의 주의를 제대로 끌지 못함 → 비공식적 시간 계획, 정중히 주의 집중 요청, 제시간에 시작
- **원인:** 맥락이 불명확함 → 회의 목적과 방법 충분히 설명
- **원인:** 주제가 그룹과 관련 없음 → 기획 단계에서 그룹 필요 사항 검토

---

### 2. Group doesn't respond to questions (그룹이 질문에 반응하지 않음)

- **원인:** 진행자와 신뢰 부족 → 따뜻하고 존중하는 말로 시작, 경청, 답이 없음을 설명, 소개 대화 진행
- **원인:** 주제가 초점에서 벗어남 → 대화의 구체적 목표 명확히 제시
- **원인:** 추상적인 시작점 → 구체적인 시작점 명확히 하고 객관적 질문 구체화
- **원인:** 질문 순서가 맞지 않아 자연스러운 사고 방해 → "잠깐, 다른 질문을 먼저 하겠습니다"라고 말하고 다른 질문으로 대체

---

## 페이지 62 (SKU-000671170_11_00062.jpeg)

# Leading a Focused Conversation 49

## Commonly Reported Troubles | Possible Reasons | Possible Solutions

### 2. Group doesn't respond to questions (continued)

| Possible Reasons | Possible Solutions |
|---|---|
| Questions are not open-ended; group senses it is being led towards a conclusion not its own | Rephrase question in an open-ended way in *Jeopardy* style — "What is..." |
| Questions are moving too fast; or moving too slow — like beads on a string. | Note where the group is, drop or add questions that will help the group get the information it needs and arrive at the decision point. |
| Many people prefer to be silent in the large group | Ask for "someone who hasn't said anything for awhile", or call by name — "Ali, what are you thinking?" Judiciously use small groups. |
| People don't feel safe in the group (an authority figure such as the teacher or principal present, etc.) | Change the overall composition of the group: break the group up into smaller groups of like people until they are more confident. |
| People shy | Go around the group on the first objective question or so to get everyone's voice out on something safe to build confidence. |
| Situation too formal | Try changing the setting to be more informal, such as sitting down. |
| Group is allergic to "touchy-feely stuff" | Ask reflective level questions with care so that people do not have to use feeling words to answer: for example, "what part of this upset you the most?" or "what immediate associations did you have with this?" (but do not leave out the reflective level altogether) |

---

## 페이지 63 (SKU-000671170_11_00063.jpeg)

# THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS (p.50)

## 자주 보고되는 문제 해결 표

### 3. 그룹이 잘못된 답변을 제시할 때
- **가능한 원인**: 진행자에게 숨겨진 의도가 있음 / 질문 순서가 맞지 않음 / 질문이 충분히 명확하지 않음
- **가능한 해결책**: 진행자 역할 상기 / 질문 순서 재조정 / 샘플 답변 제시

### 4. 그룹이 진실된 답변을 하지 않을 때
- **가능한 원인**: 그룹 구성원들이 안전하지 않다고 느낌 / 질문이 충분히 구체적이지 않음 / 참가자들에게 숨겨진 의도가 있음
- **가능한 해결책**: 덜 개인적인 주제로 시작 / 사전에 질문 테스트 / 숨겨진 의도가 드러날 수 있는 구체적 질문 제시

---

## 페이지 64 (SKU-000671170_11_00064.jpeg)

# Leading a Focused Conversation 51

## 표: 일반적인 문제 및 해결책 (Commonly Reported Troubles)

---

### 5. Some participants dominate

| Possible Reasons | Possible Solutions |
|---|---|
| Perhaps they don't feel they have been heard | Listen seriously, demonstrate "hearing them" by writing, or active listening, and respectfully cutting off — "I think we've got your main point. I'd love to hear more at the break. Now, somebody else." |
| The leader who is charged with responsibility for the outcome doesn't trust the group | Take the leader aside, check for concerns, let him know what his choices are and the consequences of dominating; address his concerns in the process. |
| Conversation style may be too open | Go around the room on a few questions. Ask each person to respond succinctly. |
| Imbalance between people who are quiet, and those who love to express in a group | Use small groups, mixing quiet and expressive together, then have groups report their results to the larger group. |

---

### 6. Group goes off on tangents

| Possible Reasons | Possible Solutions |
|---|---|
| Group wants to escape topic or responsibility | Reflect with the group about what's happening (use a focused conversation). |
| Topic is unfocused, or deemed irrelevant | Respectfully, but firmly, bring the group back to the subject. Refocus the context and the questions (See also: 1. "Group Isn't Focusing") |
| Facilitator is too wishy-washy | Remind yourself that facilitating is not about being liked, but about getting the group to handle its concerns. (See also: 1. "Group Isn't Focusing") |

---

### 7. Not getting useful results

| Possible Reasons | Possible Solutions |
|---|---|
| Topic not deemed relevant by group | See above: 1. "Group Isn't Focusing". |
| Topic is too big to be dealt with adequately in one conversation | Design several conversations on different parts of the topic. |
| Questions are not getting out useful information | Check prepared questions. Work backward from needed result to what data is needed at each level. |

---

## 페이지 65 (SKU-000671170_11_00065.jpeg)

# 52 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

## 표: 흔히 보고되는 문제들 (8-9번)

| 흔히 보고되는 문제 | 가능한 원인 | 가능한 해결책 |
|---|---|---|
| **8. Arguments break out** | 서로의 관점에 대한 존중 맥락 미형성, 서로 경청하는 방법 모름 | 다차원적 현실의 강한 맥락 수립; 첫 번째 발언자가 끝난 후 개입하여 다른 관점 질문; 상충되는 응답의 근본 패턴 질문 |
| | 진행자가 편견을 보임 | 모든 답변을 받고 있는지 자기 점검; "나는 호기심이 있다" 상기 |
| | 학생들이 논쟁 능력으로 교사에게 인상 남기려 함 | 논쟁이 아닌 사려 깊은 경청과 참여에 점수가 있음을 상기 |
| **9. Group challenges the facilitator** | 진행자가 자신감을 전달하지 못함 | 신중하게 생각된 과정을 사용하고 있으며 어떤 답도 강요하지 않음을 처음에 알림 |
| | 진행자가 전문가와 질문자의 혼재된 메시지 전달 | 개방성 확보를 위해 질문 점검 |
| | 그룹이 진행자의 권력에 과민반응 | 질문을 눈에 보이는 곳에 작성하거나 다른 사람이 대화를 이끌도록 역할 분리 |

# Introduction

*Any activity that requires higher order thinking and reasoning is likely to result in more transfer of knowledge to long-term memory and incorporation into memory in meaningful ways.*
David Carter-Todd

**주요 내용 요약:**

- 여러 나라의 현직 교사와 교육자들이 설계한 대화 모음
- 다양한 상황에 적용 가능하도록 편집됨
- **Focused Conversation** 방법을 교실, 학교, 학부모 모임에서 활용하기 위한 템플릿

- 6~9장은 참여자별(학생, 교사/직원, 학부모/지역사회)로 세분화

# Introduction to Part III — Page 55

## Chapter 7. *Making Communication Effective in Groups*

Examples are provided of conversations for student group projects, staff meetings, and parent councils which foster timely and effective communication, build shared understanding and respect, and allow a group of people to come to wise decisions.

## Chapter 8. *Preventing and Solving Problems*

These conversations demonstrate how the conversation method can be used by students, teachers, administrators, and parents to resolve misunderstandings and conflicts and resolve difficult problems, in one-on-one and group situations.

## Chapter 9. *Evaluating Learning*

The examples in this chapter provide conversations for self-evaluation, as well as evaluating performance and accomplishments of both students and staff in an effective and fair manner that allows for growth.

## Chapter 10. *Creative Applications*

In this chapter are some specific examples of creative applications of the four-level process...

---

### **How to find a helpful conversation template**

- ***Topics of interest:*** The list of conversation topics in the table of contents may spark applications that you will find helpful...

- ***Conversations designed for your audience:*** Look for similar conversations for more than one audience for clues to adapting the questions to your audience...

- ***Similar starting points:*** Sometimes there are several conversations on a similar topic that have differing beginning points...

- ***Similar aims and objectives:*** Sometimes the kind of outcome you want is more a more important similarity to look for in a sample conversation than the topic...

# 56 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

ple, if you want recommendations for responding to a policy, the topic of the policy does not affect the questions very much. The questions explore the significance of whatever policy you are focusing on, and bring out the group's recommendations.

**Using the preparation form**

When you find a template or set of templates you can use, turn to the Focused Conversation preparation form in the Appendix. Use the instructions in Chapter Four to plan your own aims, opening, closing, and questions, copying the appropriate parts from the templates. Don't forget to test your conversation by imagining your group answering the questions you have written. You may well need to adapt parts to fit the language or style of your group.

## 페이지 70 (SKU-000671170_11_00070.jpeg)

# Chapter 6

## Making Learning Meaningful

The conversations in this chapter are designed to deepen learning and help students and adults apply it to their lives.

Some of the model conversations are designed to help students — to understand assignments, to explore the deeper meaning of a topic, or to relate learning to real life applications.

Other conversations are to help teachers and parents — to reflect on events and activities, to draw out insight, and to explore new views or ideas.

## 페이지 71 (SKU-000671170_11_00071.jpeg)

# 58 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

## Reflecting on a Field Trip to the Zoo

**Designed for**
Students – Elementary

**Situation**
A class of first grade students has returned from a field trip to the zoo. They sit in a circle on the rug for a quick conversation.

**Rational Aim**
To remember the many kinds of animals they saw.

**Experiential Aim**
To experience appreciation of themselves and the world.

---

### THE CONVERSATION

**Opening**
Let's discuss our trip to the zoo.

**Objective Questions**
What animals did you see?

**Reflective Questions**
What was your favorite animal? Why?

**Interpretive Questions**
What are some similarities between children and animals? Differences?
Why was this a good trip?

**Decisional Questions**
What animals would you like to see again?
What animal would you like to learn more about?

**Closing**
Draw your favorite animal and we will hang the pictures on a zoo mural outside the classroom.

---

## 페이지 72 (SKU-000671170_11_00072.jpeg)

# Learning from a Puppet Show

---

**Designed for**
Students – Elementary

**Situation**
A class of second grade students has gone on a field trip to a puppet show of *Rumpelstiltskin* at the theater. Afterward they sit in a circle to talk about the play.

**Rational Aim**
To have the students remember the story of *Rumpelstiltskin* and to learn about honesty.

**Experiential Aim**
To experience the fun of watching puppets tell a story. To be reflective about their own lives.

---

## THE CONVERSATION

**Opening**
Let's think about how the puppet show ended.

**Objective Questions**
- Who were the characters?
- What costumes did they wear?
- What scenes do you remember?
- What were some of the lines you heard?

**Reflective Questions**
- Where were you excited?
- When did you want to cheer?
- Where did you become afraid?

**Interpretive Questions**
- What did you learn about honesty?
- What did you learn about making promises you can't keep?

**Decisional Questions**
- What can you do to remind yourself about being honest?

---

## 페이지 73 (SKU-000671170_11_00073.jpeg)

# Reflecting on a Story about Self-image

**Designed for**
Students – Elementary

**Situation**
Third grade students are studying a story in reading class called *The Golden Eagle*. The teacher wants the students to understand their reading, but also to use the message in the literature to change their own lives.

**Rational Aim**
To understand that you are what you believe you are.

**Experiential Aim**
To no longer be stuck in an old image of one's self.

---

## THE CONVERSATION

**Opening**
Let's read the story of *The Golden Eagle* out loud.

**Objective Questions**
- What words or phrases do you remember?
- What characters were in the story?
- What were some pictures that came to mind as you heard the story?
- What were some of the differences between the chicken and the eagle that the story pointed out?

**Reflective Questions**
- What emotions did you experience, for example, scared, happy, sad, or safe?
- What character seemed a lot like you?
- What did this story remind you of — things that have happened to you, things in school, things at home, things from stories or TV or movies?

**Interpretive Questions**
- How did the eagle feel about himself? Why?
- How did the chicken feel? Why?
- How did what they believed affect who they were?
- What was the message of the story?

**Decisional Questions**
- What would you want to change in your life after hearing the story?

**Closing**
Whenever you find you are getting stuck, close your eyes for a moment, and imagine yourself as a golden eagle. See what difference it makes.

---

## 페이지 74 (SKU-000671170_11_00074.jpeg)

# Reflecting on a Story — An Alternative Point of View

*Making Learning Meaningful 61*

---

## 左側 패널

**Designed for**
Students – Elementary

**Situation**
Students in a grade two class are listening to an alternative version of a fairy tale, and the teacher would like them to discuss the messages in the story.

**Rational Aim**
To understand the story and learn about alternative points of view on a story.

**Experiential Aim**
To be able to question points of view when given other stories.

**Hints**
You could follow this conversation with a writing assignment where students rewrite another fairy tale or a familiar story from one or more alternative viewpoints.

---

## THE CONVERSATION

### Opening
Class, today we are going to listen to a story — a fairy tale. There is something different about this story. We've already heard another version of this story. Let's listen, and then we are going to talk about it. *(Read the story.)*

### Objective Questions
- Who were the characters in this story?
- What happened at the beginning of the story? The middle? The end?

### Reflective Questions
- Which character did you like the best?
- What part of the story did you laugh at?
- Have you ever had a situation where no one listened to your version of what happened?
- Give an example. How did you feel?

### Interpretive Questions
- Why did you like the character you liked?
- What was the story about?
- How was this story different from the usual version of the fairy tale?
- Why do you think the wolf wrote this story?

### Decisional Questions
- What have you learned from this version of the story?
- What will you think about the next time you read a story?

### Closing
A story sometimes has two or even more sides.

---

## 페이지 75 (SKU-000671170_11_00075.jpeg)

# 62 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

## Reflecting on a Story about Something That is Not What it Seems

| **Designed for** | **THE CONVERSATION** |
|---|---|
| Students – Elementary | |

**Situation**
A sixth grade class has just read a short story where the sound of a monster in the fog turned out to be something ordinary and familiar.

**Rational Aim**
To introduce students to a short story and have them discuss the merits of the story.

**Experiential Aim**
To experience that the unknown is not always something to fear.

---

**Opening**
Let's see what this story can tell us.

**Objective Questions**
- Where does the story take place?
- Who are the main characters?
- What happens first? Then? After that?

**Reflective Questions**
- What images came to your mind as I read the story?
- What part was scary?
- What did you find funny?
- Who did you identify with?

**Interpretive Questions**
- Where have you experienced something like this?
- How would you talk about what a monster is?
- What do you think the author was trying to get across to the readers?
- What makes those important messages?

**Decisional Questions**
- How has this story changed you, or your thinking?

**Closing**
Your homework assignment is to write a short story about something or someone who is not what they seem at first.

## 페이지 76 (SKU-000671170_11_00076.jpeg)

# Responding to a Play

*Making Learning Meaningful 63*

---

**Designed for**
Students – Elementary

**Situation**
A group of children with a wide range of ages has gone to see a live presentation of *Peter Pan*. Some think it's "baby stuff."

**Rational Aim**
To recall facts, gather new ideas and use higher-level thinking skills.

**Experiential Aim**
To respect each other's insights.

---

## THE CONVERSATION

**Opening**
Let's reflect on what we saw at the play.

**Objective Questions**
- What's one thing you saw or heard in the play?
- Who were the characters?
- What were the events in the story? What happened? Where did they go? What did they do?

**Reflective Questions**
- How did you feel when Peter Pan flew through the air?
- What was a scary part of the play?
- What part did you laugh at?

**Interpretive Questions**
- How did Peter and Wendy feel about each other?
- How did they feel about the other children?
- What act would you interpret as an act of love?
- What act would you interpret as hate?
- Why did Captain Hook act like he did?
- Why did Peter do what he did?
- If you were in this story, who would you be? Why?

**Decisional Questions**
- What was this story all about?
- If you had to write another ending to this story, what would you write?
- What would you name this story, in your own words?

**Closing**
*Peter Pan* is a play for all age levels. We can all learn something from it.

---

## 페이지 77 (SKU-000671170_11_00077.jpeg)

# 64 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

## Extending a Math Exercise in Elementary School

**Designed for**
Students – Elementary

**Situation**
The teacher has just involved the children in an exercise manipulating toothpicks to show first 2 + 2 and the total number, then groups of 2 and the total number; then 3 + 3 and the total number, and two groups of 3 and the total number. Students leave the groupings on their desks in front of them.

**Rational Aim**
To understand that multiplication is a shortcut for addition — that "times" means "groups of".

**Experiential Aim**
To realize "Aha! I can do this!"

---

### THE CONVERSATION

**Opening**
We're going to see what we can learn from what we just did.

**Objective Questions**
Describe what you see on your desk.

**Reflective Questions**
What surprises you?

**Interpretive Questions**
How would you describe the pattern you see?

What have you discovered?

How is multiplying similar to adding? How is it different?

Why do you think the total of 3 + 3 is the same as the total of 2 groups of 3?

This is how we can write this: 3 + 3 = 6, or 3 x 2 = 6

**Decisional Questions**
How would you summarize what you see on your desk? Answer by drawing it and write the math sentences beneath your pictures.

Try again with 4 + 4 + 4 and 3 groups of 4, first with the toothpicks, then drawing them.

Write this in two ways.

Try it first alone, then we'll do it together.

**Closing**
Wow! You're doing multiplication! Whenever you do "a number of groups of" a number of things, you're doing multiplication.

Hand out assignment sheet for homework, extending the exercise.

---

## 페이지 78 (SKU-000671170_11_00078.jpeg)

# Making Learning Meaningful 65

## Integrating the Experience of the Day

| | |
|---|---|
| **Designed for** | **THE CONVERSATION** |
| Students – Elementary | |
| **Situation** | **Opening** |
| In an elementary classroom, a teacher reflects with her class daily at the end of the day. | Just before the bell rings, let's wind up the day with a quick reflection. |
| **Rational Aim** | **Objective Questions** |
| To create a deeper understanding of the implications of the day. To be conscious of learning from experiences. | What is something that happened today — in class or on the playground? What is something you saw? What is something someone said? What are some sounds you heard? |
| **Experiential Aim** | **Reflective Questions** |
| To experience the day as complete. To be affirmed in the day's challenges. | What was something funny, or something sad that you experienced today? What made you excited or frustrated? What were some of the feelings you saw others having today? |
| **Hints** | **Interpretive Questions** |
| There are lots of questions here. Usually you ask only one or two questions at each level, depending on time and flow. | Where did you experience something that went really well today? What did not go so well for you? What was really appropriate behavior? What was really inappropriate behavior? What was something that didn't happen today that you wanted to have happen? Where did you see learning happening today? Where did you see someone's needs being met? Where did you have a breakthrough? Where did you achieve something in spite of the problems? Where did you experience individual success? Where did you see a real team effort? What would you say this class was about today? |
| **Other Applications** | **Decisional Questions** |
| Solitary conversation for journaling. End of session, a unit of work, or term. Daily debriefing for a group on a work/study tour to another culture. | What would you have changed about today if you could? What would you have kept the same? |

*(continued)*

---

## 페이지 79 (SKU-000671170_11_00079.jpeg)

# 66 THE ART OF FOCUSED CONVERSATION FOR SCHOOLS

## Integrating the Experience of the Day *(continued)*

| | |
|---|---|
| | If you could take away everything from this day except one event or experience, which event or experience would that be? |
| | If you had to explain to somebody from "outer space" what you did today, what would you say? |
| | How would you draw a picture of this day? |
| | What rating between 1 and 10 would you give to how good this day was for you? |
| | What name would you give to this day? "This was the day of…" |
| | What do you need to do as a result of this day? |
| | What changes can you make for tomorrow? Next week? |
| | **Closing** |
| | Thanks for sharing your reflections about the day. A lot happened in a few short hours. I've learned a lot from what you have said. |
